### [223] [Stigmergic Swarming Agents for Fast Subgraph Isomorphism](https://arxiv.org/abs/2601.02449)
*H. Van Dyke Parunak*

Main category: cs.MA

TL;DR: ASSIST (Approximate Swarming Subgraph Isomorphism through Stigmergy) is a heuristic inspired by ant colony optimization for solving the NP-complete problem of maximum partial subgraph isomorphism. It achieves near-linear time complexity for the combinatorially complex part of the problem after an initial peering step.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The problem of finding a largest common subgraph between two graphs is NP-complete, with existing solutions being computationally expensive or impractical for large graphs. ASSIST aims to provide a faster and more efficient heuristic inspired by ant colony optimization.

Method: ASSIST uses an ant colony optimization approach, starting with a peering step to match individual nodes ($O(q \cdot log(d))$ time). The iterative subgraph search, the core of the problem, is linear in query size and constant in data size.

Result: ASSIST significantly reduces the time complexity for the combinatorial part of the problem compared to existing heuristics, achieving near-linear performance. It also supports extended matching problems like inexact matches or missing elements.

Conclusion: ASSIST offers a scalable and efficient solution for maximum partial subgraph isomorphism, outperforming current heuristics and accommodating complex matching scenarios.

Abstract: Maximum partial subgraph isomorphism compares two graphs (nodes joined by edges) to find a largest common subgraph. A common use case, for graphs with labeled nodes, seeks to find instances of a \textit{query} graph with $q$ nodes in a (typically larger) \textit{data} graph with $d$ nodes. The problem is NP-complete, and na√Øve solutions are exponential in $q + d$. The fastest current heuristic has complexity $O(d^2)$. This paper outlines ASSIST (Approximate Swarming Subgraph Isomorphism through Stigmergy), inspired by the ant colony optimization approach to the traveling salesperson. After peering (identifying matching individual nodes in query and data) in time $O(q\cdot log(d))$, the time required for ASSIST's iterative subgraph search, the combinatorially complex part of the problem, is linear in query size and constant in data size. ASSIST can be extended to support matching problems (such as temporally ordered edges, inexact matches, and missing nodes or edges in the data graph) that frustrate other heuristics.

</details>


### [224] [Modellierung und Simulation der Dynamik von Fussg√§ngerstr√∂men](https://arxiv.org/abs/2601.02526)
*P√©ter Moln√°r*

Main category: cs.MA

TL;DR: A microscopic pedestrian flow model using social force theory shows how simple individual behaviors create complex collective patterns, influenced by building geometry, and includes optimization methods for infrastructure design.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To develop a realistic pedestrian flow model for designing pedestrian-friendly infrastructure and verify social science theories with empirical data.

Method: Combines social force theory with individual behavior rules (goal-directed movement and distance maintenance), integrates an evolutionary algorithm for building layout optimization, and includes decision-making and adaptation models.

Result: Demonstrates emergent collective behaviors like trail formation, strong dependency on building geometry, and efficiency improvements through geometric adjustments.

Conclusion: The model effectively captures pedestrian dynamics, optimizes infrastructure design, and enhances understanding of self-organized systems in pedestrian flows.

Abstract: This work presents a microscopic model to describe pedestrian flows based on the social force theory. The aim of this study is twofold: (1) developing a realistic model that can be used as a tool for designing pedestrian-friendly infrastructure, and (2) verifying a social science theory using a model with sufficient data. The investigation of the pedestrian model shows that despite simple individual behavior patterns, complex spatial and temporal structures emerge through the interactions in pedestrian flows. Collective behavior emerges from individuals following two basic rules: (1) moving directly towards their goal at a certain speed, and (2) maintaining a distance to other pedestrians and obstacles. This self-organized collective behavior manifests itself as trails that are formed by pedestrians moving in one direction. Furthermore, strong dependencies of the properties of pedestrian flows on geometric forms of buildings are shown, and the influence of geometric changes on performance characteristics is investigated. An example demonstrates how efficiency can be increased by reducing walkable areas. This work also presents an evolutionary algorithm for optimizing building layouts based on the social force model. Additionally, a decision-making model is integrated to describe alternative goal selection, and adaptation and learning capabilities are included to improve pedestrian avoidance behavior and decision strategies based on accumulated experience. A method for determining load distributions in individual sections of a path system considering subjective selection criteria is also developed. Finally, a model that describes the self-organization of path systems with minimal detours is presented, similar to natural transport networks where total length and material costs are optimized.

</details>
